# Learning Data Analytics: 1 Foundations

> Are you interested in pursuing a career in data analytics? In this course, instructor Robin Hunt brings you into the mind of an analyst. She defines and explains foundational concepts, such as how to think about data, how to work with others in different roles to get the data you need, and the tools you need to work with data, such as Excel and Microsoft Access. She introduces you to SQL queries, 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations) | 3h 29m | Beginner | 22K learners

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/beginning-your-data-analysis-journey-23310612?u=76281980&t=7)** That's where data analysis comes in.
>

>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=4)** However, to get the most out of it you should have a pretty good working knowledge of Excel.

> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=26)** Even if you cannot directly perform the commands there's a lot of value in understanding the concepts and applying them to tools that are created to work with data.

### 1. Getting Started with Data Analysis

#### Defining data analysis and data analyst
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When people ask me to speak about data or find out that I work in data, I know some of their first questions will be, "Well, what is data and what is a data analyst?"
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=10)** I know after the last two decades of being asked this, they want a single sentence answer that's easy to digest.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=18)** Unfortunately, even though the word data was first used in 1646, there's really not a single sentence answer.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=26)** So I typically lean towards a deeper, more practical conversation to what data means to them, so I can determine what is the best way to answer their questions.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=36)** The world of data is so broad, and in my humble opinion, I think it will remain broad because we're still in the early days of discovery and tools development.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=46)** With this course, I can provide more than one sentence to explain what data analysis is.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=52)** And I think we should start by breaking down the words data and analysis.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=57)** Let's tackle the biggest four-letter word in the universe, data.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=61)** Even the dictionaries have a hard time giving a practical definition of data, because again, it can mean a lot of different things.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=69)** And for the data analyst of the world, we need to look at two of the definitions.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=74)** Factual information, such as measurements or statistics, used as a basis for reasoning, discussion, or calculation, and information in digital form that can be transmitted or processed.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=88)** Now let's break down analysis.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=91)** It's been my experience that when you say analysis or analyze, people immediately focus on specific types of analysis like statistical or mathematical analysis, especially when thinking of data.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=104)** And even though this is certainly true for data, the reality is analysis is an everyday part of life for a data worker, and it's broader than one type of analysis.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=115)** I love Merriam Webster's definition of analysis, "A detailed examination of anything complex in order to understand its nature or determine its essential features; a thorough study."
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=127)** And this definition has been around since about 1581.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=132)** Now that we're on the same page about analysis, let's look at the word analyze.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=136)** And to me, this is the perfect definition of what we do in the world of data at almost every level, "To study or determine the nature and relationship of the parts of something by analysis."
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=150)** To describe what a data analyst is, and at the foundational level, I've combined these definitions together to give you the best possible definition.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/defining-data-analysis-and-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=159)** A data analyst is someone who will study or determine the nature and relationships of factual information used as a basis for reasoning, discussion, or calculation by always doing a detailed examination of information in digital form in order to understand its nature or to determine its essential features.

>

#### Discovering if you are an analyst
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Do you have a fitness tracker or a calorie counter of some sort?
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=4)** If you said yes, then you're already gathering data.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=8)** Do you walk more when you notice your steps are low?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=11)** Do you eat more or less based on what you did earlier in the day or yesterday?
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=16)** Are you using your history to determine what you should do next?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=20)** Do you set a target on your calories and do you monitor your calories and make adjustments to stay within your goals for the day?
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=28)** Then you're already using data for decision-making, which means you're already analyzing data.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=33)** This is just a practical example of how we all capture and analyze data, but data analysts take these skills and go even further.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=42)** For years, I suggested that there are far more people that are working as data analysts than we might believe based on their job titles.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=50)** We did a small local study where we asked people to identify if they were data workers, and then we asked them things like their job titles.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=57)** We asked people to self-identify if they were data workers based on the European Data Market Study definition who are workers who collect, store, manage and analyze data as their primary activity, or as a relevant part of their activity.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=74)** We then analyzed their job titles to determine if job titles could and should be used as an indication of who's a data analyst.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=83)** Overwhelmingly the response from people who considered themselves data workers in organizations of all sizes and all industries did not have the literal words data analyst in their job titles.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=98)** Because we are data analysts, we searched deeper into the titles for the words data or analyst, and even though we found the word analyst was more prevalent, even then, 73% who considered themselves data workers had no occurrence of data analyst, or data or analyst in their titles, and their titles were all sorts of different things.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=124)** Even more interesting to me was that even if they did not describe themselves as a data worker, they were still performing data-related tasks and processes and using data-related software.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=138)** As data has grown and been adopted by organizations around the globe, more people are using data to provide information to their leadership, their departments, and they report outcomes even if it's just historical.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=152)** People are performing key tasks, even on the smallest of datasets.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=156)** They're inspecting, cleaning, transforming and modeling data for reports that are consumed weekly, monthly, or even annual.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=165)** Ask yourself, are you exporting data out to Excel or CSV to work with it?
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=171)** I would call these flat files.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=173)** Finding and accessing data to work with it is something that a data analyst does on every project.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=179)** When you get that data out of the system or systems, do you ever delete columns or add calculations that your system doesn't provide?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=187)** This is a form of data cleaning and transformation.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=190)** It's an everyday part of life for a data analyst.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=195)** Do you build V lookups or X lookups to bring data from other worksheets or other files?
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=200)** If you are, then you're using joins and modeling datasets.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=204)** Are you then building pivots, crosstabs, charts or graphs?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=208)** This would be an example of the visualization of information.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=212)** Does this sound like you?
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=214)** Data analysts perform some or all of these tasks at any given time or all of the time.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=220)** Data analysts find, collect, and/or capture data.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=223)** They inspect it, they transform it.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=226)** They add functions and formulas, and then they merge data sets so they can then further analyze it with pivots, charts and other visualizations.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-if-you-are-an-analyst?u=76281980&t=235)** All data has a purpose and the data analyst is typically seeking answers to the questions and then performing tasks like cleaning and transforming data to provide insight and informed decision-making.

#### Organizational roles in data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] It's important to understand the organizational roles of data analysis from a high level.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=5)** Again, there are many interpretation of these roles.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=8)** It varies by organization based on what they do, the size of their data, their workforce and who they serve.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=16)** For example, a company that serves 1,000 individuals will have a different strategy and maybe different roles than an organization that is highly regulated and serves millions of people.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=27)** There isn't a one-size-fits-all strategy for any given organization, but there are some key organizational roles that are sometimes filled by departments, committees or even individuals.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=40)** I would like to focus on four key areas that you might find.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=44)** No matter how small or how large an organization is, these are some pretty major areas.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=51)** Research, research helps define the questions to be answered, helps establish parameters of your data, and in this role, you will find people who specialize in key areas of the business.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=64)** They're focused on methods, and they also spend time with statistical outcomes.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=70)** Data governance is as much a process as it is a role, but let's focus on the organizational role of data governance.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=78)** This role ensures that ownership and accountability of data is clearly defined.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=84)** It also ensures that people in the organization who need access are allowed access to the data.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=91)** It's also a role that should cross all departments of an organization, not just siloed inside of an IT department.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=99)** We'll talk about the ways that data governance impacts you as an analyst later in this course.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=105)** Technology most certainly plays a role in all things in an organization.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=110)** Organizations have technology, not only in what they do or produce, but also in their back office operations.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=118)** Technology roles in data are vast.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=120)** They may include server infrastructure for data, the security of data, most certainly.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=125)** And in some cases, they control all access to the data as a part of the data governance plans.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=131)** They should be actively involved in anything related to data governance.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=136)** Data in an organization from an organizational role can be found at every level, from end-user to dedicated data and analysis department.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=147)** Some organizations that are truly dealing with big data, which grows every day, have fully dedicated data departments, where they have data scientists, data engineers, data analysts dedicated to the data of the organization.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=162)** When an organization doesn't have a dedicated data department, you will likely find these roles are being served within the technology department.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/organizational-roles-in-data?u=76281980&t=171)** The organizational role of data for any company is obvious, improve their product, improve their service, improve their bottom line, and hopefully solve some worldwide problems.

#### Discovering skills of the data analyst
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If a carpenter is starting a woodworking project, they don't just show up with a hammer.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=5)** They have a set of tools at their disposal.
>

> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=17)** A seasoned carpenter is not only leveraging tools but also leveraging their experiences over time.

>

>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=131)** Anyone can make numbers show up.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=133)** So understanding and communicating where you found the data and the quality of the data is critical.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=140)** Is it keyed directly in by customer who makes an order?
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=144)** Does shipping complete their shipping through a system or are they telling someone who tracks it on a spreadsheet?
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=150)** It's important to identify the quality of the data and learn things about what impacts data quality.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=157)** Another key skill is to determine what data is important to answering the specific question.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=163)** All too often, you have access to more columns of data than what you need to answer a specific question.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=169)** This is important because it helps define what cleaning and transformation you will need to do and exactly how much of it you will need to do.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=178)** You'll also need to able to create valid data through calculations.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=183)** In a perfect world, everything we need exists already, but we don't live in a perfect world.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=188)** Hear me out and don't stop just because I said something that sounds like math.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=193)** Here's an example.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=194)** We have a date that something was ordered.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=197)** We have a date in which it was shipped.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=200)** But our dataset doesn't specifically state the number of days between.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=205)** We can easily use those two dates to write a function that tells us the number of days from order to shipped.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=213)** The scenario I presented here could be accomplished using any tools dedicated to data.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=219)** I think a major skill for any data analysts, and one that's really lacking for most data analysts, is presenting the information in a clear and understandable format.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=229)** This doesn't mean explaining orders and shipping data and the fact that it's stored in two different systems and that you had several menial steps and several really highly complicated steps.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=241)** It means did you answer the question with a visual that is actionable and actually answers the question.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=249)** As you grow more seasoned, you might learn more data tools where you will expand into deeper data skills and you might even pick up coding skills.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=259)** However, if you cannot create or survive a pivot table, you should really start there.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=266)** Datasets will continue to grow.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=268)** And new datasets are always being created.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=271)** So the skills you need will continue to grow and change over time.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=276)** Just remember that not that long ago tools like Power BI and Tableau didn't exist yet, but building a core foundation on working with data will allow you to work successfully with any tool that is built for data.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=291)** As we grow more innovative, data will continue to grow.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-skills-of-the-data-analyst?u=76281980&t=295)** And the supply and demand for people with data skills will continue to increase.

#### Learning about data fields and types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are three key things to know about a data point.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=4)** It has a field name, a datatype and a value.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=8)** What most people see as data are actually values.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=12)** Values are what we're entering, exporting and reporting on.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=16)** If you've ever entered data on a form and it asks for first name, then you enter the value of your first name.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=23)** For me, that would be Robin and that's text.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=26)** The values for every field are a key consideration on how we define the data type.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=32)** In a perfect world, field names would be designed to be meaningful, data types would be defined based on what our brain interprets and the values would always be perfectly entered.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=42)** As you venture into the everyday life of a data analyst, you will discover the world is not perfect and data is not always, as it seems.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=52)** Field names can be a broad label of information like, First Name, Last Name, or Job Title.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=60)** We again hope that the field names are named meaningfully.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=64)** Just be aware of, that's not always the case.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=67)** People call field names, column headers and they're usually represented by the top row and typically in Excel at a minimum they're bold.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=76)** Data types drive what we can and should do with the data when we work with it.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=81)** All data has a type.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=83)** To simplify this breakdown, I want you to understand at a high level data only has a few types, it's text, numbers, or dates.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=93)** Over time, you will learn that it does go deeper than that, but we're just getting started.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=98)** When you export data out of a system, it's likely stored in a database.
>

>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=108)** You'll see the field names are created, you'll say there are data types associated to them.

>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=189)** For example, if you saw color as the field name and the value is the word red, and I ask you which one of the high level data types it is, you would probably say words or text.
>

>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=205)** If I show you a one, five or 45,981, you would say that's a number.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=212)** If you see data like 11/20/2025, as a human you might immediately interpret that as a date because you think about calendars, appointments, someone's birthday.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=224)** When you look at data and you think about what it actually is, you're actually thinking about the data type.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=230)** Data analysts have grown to understand that this is where it starts to get tricky what our eyes see and what the program says is not always the same thing.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=241)** When we work with data in Excel or in databases, and that data is defined by the systems, what you see isn't exactly how it is defined or stored.
>

>

>

> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=296)** So I'll type my 1/4.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=300)** Again, I mean one quarter.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=302)** Excel immediately interprets this as January 4th.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=307)** The minute Excel believes that you mean a date, it formats that information as a date.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=314)** So you actually meant a fraction and Excel returns a date.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=319)** Don't worry, it's not just happening to you, the scientists that study human genes have renamed approximately 27 human genes due to the fact that Excel misread genes like March 1 and Sep one as March 1st and September 1st.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=337)** What's important to understand is that, what Excel is actually doing is it's formatting what it believes is a serial number to display a date.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=347)** Because of the format, we see the human version of the date.
>
> **[5:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=352)** Why?
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=353)** Again, it's because Excel converts dates to serial numbers so it can use them mathematically.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=359)** To correct this formatting, you have to go and change the format back to a number or general.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=366)** And for some people this is the maximum exposure to data type.
>
> **[6:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=369)** Let me show you how to do that.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=372)** So I'll click this sale, I'll go to the number and I'll go ahead and convert it to either general or number.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=380)** And I'll go ahead and decrease those decimals.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=385)** Common data types that data analysts should know or texts or string, date, time, numbers, and Boolean which might look like a true or false, yes or no.
>
> **[6:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=395)** If you're working in programs like Microsoft Access, it's actually a negative one or zero but then tools like SQL it's one or zero.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=404)** Different technologies have different types of data types.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=408)** For example, access databases have a very short list of data types or a SQL database will have a multitude of different types to choose from.
>
> **[6:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=417)** But at the end of the day, they still roll up to those high level dates, texts, numbers or Boolean.
>
> **[7:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=424)** The reality is that all too often the field names are not meaningful, the data types might not be what you expect and the values when keyed by humans, are not always correctly entered.
>
> **[7:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-fields-and-types?u=76281980&t=434)** However, for the up-and-coming analyst or just the existing data worker, this is a fact of life and in some cases, job security.

> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=116)** You'll see people asking similar questions to yours, and it will be full of suggestions and potential syntax.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=123)** I encourage people to use real-people terms to find the answer versus attempting to write technical terms.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=130)** Simple searches like tie names together will likely to take you to a concatenator CONCAT function.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=136)** Or convert text date to a real date in Excel will likely take you to some conversion functions.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=143)** Keep in mind that syntax requires following the rules of the syntax for that program.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=148)** The way you write a function in one program is not necessarily how you write it in all programs.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=153)** However, if you have learned syntax for one program, it's easier to find it in other programs.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=159)** For example, you could use words like, how do I concatenate in SQL or how do I write an IF function in Power BI?
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=168)** Many modern tools give us the ability to use a GUI or graphical user interface to build things.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=173)** But we can always take a peek behind the scenes to look at the syntax.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=177)** For example, in Power Query, we would look at M code.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=180)** In Macros, we would look at the VBA.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=183)** In the Function palette, we would just look at what the function is written in the formula bar.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=188)** Even Access queries build a SQL statement that you can see in the background and learn how to read.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=194)** There's no way to get around learning syntax.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=196)** And if you want to expand the capabilities of your data and your skills, the more you learn about syntax of any particular program, the more you can leverage it.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-syntax?u=76281980&t=205)** If you find you're in love with syntax, if you like to type out all that code versus using the GUI, you might grow up to pursue data science, where programming is one of the key differences between a data scientist and a data analyst.

> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-basic-sql-statements?u=76281980&t=346)** There are some consistency in the statements like the SELECT and FROM and the WHERE in ORDER BY.

### 3. Key Elements to Understand when Starting Data Analysis

#### Learning to interpret existing data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Most companies have reports today that they use for decision making.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=4)** It could be two weeks old or a month old when they get it, but it is the report that they use.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=10)** Learning about data from existing reports can help you get ahead of the learning curve of the data.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=15)** It also teaches you about the business rules you may need to know.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=19)** Let me back up a little bit and give you some concepts that I think will help you look at reports a little bit differently.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=25)** It's also important to keep in mind that the way we see the data displayed is not always have the data is stored.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=32)** Something that most people can relate to is a menu.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=35)** We can all appreciate a menu at our favorite place.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=39)** A menu really operates as a report of that restaurant.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=43)** We use it to make a decision about what they're offering that day, what we want to eat and how much money we want to spend.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=50)** If you really think about a menu, it has different categories of information like small plates, beverages, main dishes and desserts.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=59)** These are just simple examples of what we might find on a menu.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=62)** Let's focus on the main dishes for now.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=66)** The chef has created the main dishes from recipes that are either standard to that restaurant or created directly by the chef.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=73)** No matter who creates those recipes, the dishes represent ingredients, measurements of those ingredients and preparation rules.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=82)** The chef knows that in order to offer that dish, they need the ingredients and the rules to follow.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=87)** That way they have exactly what they need in the right amounts, mixing them at the right time and cooking them at the correct temperatures.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=95)** And at the same time, the pastry chef is thinking through exactly what they will need to make that award-winning dessert that just lands in another place on the same menu.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=105)** The ingredients are different.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=107)** The requirements are different, but they do end up on the same final display.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=112)** At some point, the chef will determine it's time for them to shop for the ingredients.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=116)** And the chef might visit different aisles and different stores to gather the ingredients for the dishes that will be on the menu.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=123)** There will be time when the chef tastes different ingredients and mixes them together to create another ingredient for the recipe.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=131)** In data, the menu is the report.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=135)** The ingredients are the data, and the measurements and prep rules are examples of the business rules.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=142)** When reviewing a report, ask yourself, what are the data points that are collected together?
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=147)** And what are those data points that you see on the report?
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=152)** Determine if those data points together are following any specific rules like only showing the last 30 days or showing the next seven days.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=161)** Have you ever been to the grocery store and forgotten your list?
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=164)** It's easy to miss a key ingredient or spend too much on ingredients you don't need.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=170)** It's no different for the data analyst.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=172)** Once you have everything documented, you're ready to start shopping for your data.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=176)** And just like the chef, you may have to shop for your data, except the aisles are tables and databases are spreadsheets.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=184)** And the different stores are different databases to get everything you need.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=189)** On every project that I start, I typically analyze the reporting that they're currently using.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=194)** That way I can see what they're using to make their decisions, determine what categories they're using, what are the data points they're looking at and what are the rules?
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-to-interpret-existing-data?u=76281980&t=204)** And then I'm ready to create my data shopping list.

#### Cleaning data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I have had the great experience of never receiving data in usable form, or what I would call clean.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=7)** I've always sort of been expected to get it cleaned up to make it usable and meaningful.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=14)** I'm grateful because it helps me appreciate how to deal with any data that I see.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=19)** What is data cleaning?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=20)** And since we're at the beginning of the journey, I'm going to keep it really simple.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=25)** It's the process of standardizing the data and making it meaningful.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=30)** If you use Excel routinely as a part of your reporting process, or if Excel is all of your reporting process, you are likely cleaning a lot.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=40)** It might include the removal of columns that are unneeded for the data model and the report.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=46)** It might mean removing extra spaces from a field using TRIM or CLEAN commands.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=52)** It might mean making all states to letter abbreviations and even making them uppercase.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=59)** You might've received data where address information is all together, but you need to break it out into individual fields.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=68)** You might also remove invalid records for the purpose of the report.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=72)** For example, you don't need all the sales that haven't completed the full process, so you remove those in process sales records.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=81)** They're valid for the sales process, but they're not valid for the type of report you're writing.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=86)** You might also remove duplicated data from a large set to create a set that's appropriate for your business case.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=94)** These are all simple types of data cleaning or cleansing, and in no way is this a comprehensive list of things you might encounter to produce that nice clean data set.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=104)** We will work with several of these commands a little bit later in the course.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=108)** It is always important that you work with one key goal in mind, which is a high quality dataset that is usable.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/cleaning-data?u=76281980&t=117)** You need to feel confident about the result and you'll need to be able to speak about the data cleaning that you applied to the data, so be sure to keep good notes.

#### Understanding data and workflow
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Every organization is different.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=2)** And then interestingly enough, the same.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=5)** There is the way that the work flows through the organization and the data that's captured around that flow.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=11)** Before I was an entrepreneur, when I was building up my industry experience and skills, I worked in a highly regulated industry and it was intense.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=20)** And my first two weeks of work, I did nothing but read policy and procedure manuals, full of workflow diagrams.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=27)** It was an amazing experience because it changed the way I looked at every process and it forced me to diagram out how the workflows in every place I would eventually go.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=38)** In every situation I always ask, is there a policy around this process?
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=43)** And do you have any procedural documentation?
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=46)** And do you happen to have a workflow?
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=49)** You may find that the procedure either does not exist in writing or it's in bits and pieces.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=55)** No matter whether they have a workflow diagram or not, I always start my own diagram so I can intelligently figure out where data is captured in any part of the process and can document where to potentially find it.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=68)** Let me walk you through a simple process.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=71)** Let's use a sales example because it gives us a lot of opportunity to look at the different parts of a process.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=78)** A sale is created, a product is ordered, a product is delivered and accounting occurs.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=86)** This sounds so simple, right?
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=88)** Let's take a look at each of those four statements and work out the timing and the data.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=93)** I'm using a swim lane diagram for this demonstration but understand there are many types of workflow diagrams.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=101)** Let's look at the point that a sales lead comes in.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=103)** This is the beginning point of this process.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=106)** It then moves to determine if a customer is new or not and if they are, they're added to the Customer Relationship Management software or CRM.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=116)** Do you see the data.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=118)** In reality, the data came in way before then.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=121)** How do we get the sales lead?
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=123)** Is that captured somewhere like our website or inside something form?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=127)** Is it from an online store?
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=130)** Was it a cold call?
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=132)** Okay, obviously we're going to look at the CRM pace to see if we can identify if the customer exists already or not.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=139)** Basically, we're trying to determine if we know about them.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=142)** Once we have that taken care of, we then move to the order being created.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=147)** For this example, we don't care what widget or service people are buying.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=152)** Ultimately, it's the product of this organization.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=155)** The sales team then works with the customer and at some point the order is created and it becomes a sales order.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=162)** Again, do you see the data?
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=164)** You might say yes, the sales order data but let me assure you it's really in the order creation that it begins.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=172)** I would ask questions like where is the data the salespeople use to show the options to the customer?
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=179)** Is that the same system that generates the sales order or is that in a different system?
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=185)** At some point the sales order then passes to the production team.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=190)** It passes to the production team for approval.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=193)** The production team then will determine if this is something that can actually be approved based on the customer's requirements.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=201)** The question on the data here is how do we send sales orders to production?
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=207)** Is it in a system?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=208)** Is it a spreadsheet?
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=210)** Is it through an email with a PDF attachment and then where is the data that holds the actual approval or the lack of approval?
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=220)** Is it the same system that holds the sales order?
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=223)** Now for time say we're going to move through the approved process which then goes back to sales to let them know that the sale has been approved and then sales then routes it to accounting to create an actual invoice in the accounting system, even more data.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=240)** And that data is likely siloed because accounting software is super specialized and shows a whole other level of organizational reporting and requirements, it's typically sensitive.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=252)** Then we go back to the sales team who then submits the invoice to the customer.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=257)** The customer then pays and routes it to accounting.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=260)** In this example, only when the customer pays does it get routed from accounting to production.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=270)** It's then routed to shipping, it's then routed directly to the customer and upon delivery, the sales kicks back in for the close out of the process.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=281)** Now that we've walked through the entire process and captured all the different places data might be, we can then look and research questions like, how long does it take us to close out a customer from the point that they paid to the point that the closeout begins.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=297)** You now have a process to follow and a flow.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=301)** If you're trying to determine timing between production to shipping.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=305)** Again, you may have identified the systems, you can then determine what happens between production and shipping.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=312)** If you're tasked to determine how many new customers versus existing customers are making orders each month and the impacts to production, you now have a path to follow.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=324)** I think it's important to know that whether the processes are documented or the workflow diagrams exist that every organization has a process, good, bad or indifferent.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=335)** And if you don't have something formal to follow, you should at least rough sketch the work.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=340)** So you can see the workflow.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-data-and-workflow?u=76281980&t=342)** Data is living and again, it occurs at points in time and through out a process.

#### Understanding joins
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the hardest concepts for new analysts is how to join data in multiple tables.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=5)** There is no single best answer that is always applicable to every situation.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=10)** If you're working with data sets that have already been joined for you, that is amazing.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=15)** But when you're given the back-end access to multiple tables, you will likely need to join them, and you'll usually do this through queries.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=22)** Another thing to keep in mind is that programs that work with multiple data sets typically provide you a way to join them.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=29)** In Excel, it's VLOOKUPs.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=31)** And in databases, it's queries.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=34)** Each data set needs a way to relate the data, something shared on both sets of the data.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=40)** In the best-case scenario, that is a key field that uniquely identifies the information, so you can use it to join.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=47)** It's worth noting that in queries, there are different types of joins, like inner, outer, and cross joins.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=54)** The type of join will determine the end result, so it's important to understand them.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=59)** The more practice you have or real-life experience you gain, the more natural it will become for you to assign joins based on the results you want to achieve.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=68)** There are a lot of people who are joining data in Excel.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=71)** They don't realize they're creating joins.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=74)** They're using VLOOKUPS to establish a join based on a key field.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=77)** They're pulling in one field at a time.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=81)** Let's talk about the different types of joins.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=84)** A cross join occurs when you include tables in a query window and do not join them with a key field.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=90)** This ties every record in the table to every record in the other table.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=95)** This could be valuable if you meant to do it, but for most people, it's just simply because they forgot to include the join.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=103)** When you have two tables that do have a join on a field, then the default join is an inner join.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=109)** That means that if a record in each table matches, it will show in your results.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=114)** An example would be if you want to see a list of sales and the products that were ordered, then an inner join would be appropriate.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=121)** Then you have left and right outer.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=124)** Left or right is determined by systems differently.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=127)** It's more important to understand about what a left or right will do in the beginning, not so much which one is left and which one is right.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=135)** Left or right outers will return every record from one table and the matching records from the other table, meaning you can see every record from one side, regardless of its matches on the other side.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=149)** So if your goal was to show a list of products and if it was ordered, that would be the appropriate join.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=157)** When you've been provided data to work with and if you've been given access to the back end, then you'll get lots of real-world experience working with joins.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-joins?u=76281980&t=166)** Every data program will provide you a way to join that data and will often guide you with information on the screen that will help you understand what your data set result will be.

#### Working with joins and validation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you join data, you were using a field of data that is the same in both tables.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=6)** And it is the same data type.
>

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=63)** Let's talk about another common mistake that produces a cross join.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=68)** Right click and open in design view the query named cross.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=74)** When you add two tables to a query window and you do not have a join type, this is called a cross join.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=81)** A cross join we'll actually take every record from one table and attach it to every record in another table.

> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=89)** Let's go ahead and get the sales order detail count and also the product count.
