>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=118)** So the record results would be 79,584 times 504 to produce over 4 million records.

> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=128)** So if you see an inflation in records, it could be that you didn't join your tables.

> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=163)** I know that this is an inner join because I do not see arrow shapes on either side of the join.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=170)** Just be aware that when you do adjust to join it will change the way the join line looks.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=175)** Now when I run this query I will say products that have ordered and have sales records.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=181)** I'll go ahead and run that.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=185)** Because I've checked my record counts, I'll say that I have all 79,584 sales records.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=193)** But what this doesn't show me is all of the products that have not been ordered.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=198)** I can only see the sales records of the products that have been ordered.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=204)** Okay, I'll go ahead and save and close this.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=209)** So if my goal was to actually report and analyze products that have not been ordered, I would need to perform an outer join.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=220)** Okay. Let's check those record counts of products one more time.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=224)** I'll open up the product table.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=226)** I do have 504 product records.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=229)** So if I'm looking at product records and I'm assuming some have not been ordered, then I can't tell it from just looking at product.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=237)** I have to actually tie it to the sales order and then filter for the ones that have not been ordered.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=243)** Okay, I'll close product.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=246)** I don't need to save any changes.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=248)** And I'll go ahead and do left outer in design view .
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=253)** To adjust the joint properties is simple, you can double click the join line, or you can right click and go to join properties.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=260)** Every program sees left and right based on different ways and reasons.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=265)** Here, I can see my left table name is product, and my right table name is sales order detail.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=272)** If for any reason I wanted to change those I could.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=276)** So let's write the three different join types.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=279)** Include rows where joining fields from both tables are equal, that's the default inner join.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=285)** Include all records from production product and only those records from sales order detail where the join fields are equal.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=295)** And because product is in the left side that would be a left outer join.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=301)** The third join type that's included here is include all records from sales order detail and only those records from production product where the joint fields are equal.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=312)** In the case of this data that would produce the same result as an inner join.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=317)** So I want option two.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=319)** I want to see all of my products and then their associated sales records.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=324)** I'll go ahead and click Okay.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=327)** So when I run that query I'll see the results of that left outer join.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=331)** Most notably you'll notice that starting at sales order ID, the fields are null and there are null all the way across.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=339)** That's because there are no records in sales for these products.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=346)** If I scroll down through my record set, the moment I find a product that's been ordered, I start to say the sales order record is no longer null.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=358)** This would give me a comprehensive list of all products and their associated sales orders.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=364)** If I only want a list of products that have never been ordered, I can then flip the design and place is null in the criteria for sales order.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=374)** Let me show you.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=376)** I'll right click and go to the design view.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=379)** I'll go to sales order and type, is null.
>
> **[6:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=384)** This particular query when I run it will give me every product that has not been ordered.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=392)** So of our 504 products, there are 246 products that have no associated sales order records.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=402)** When you're responsible for engineering your own datasets, your reporting requirement or data objective will determine what data you need to join and what type of join will be required of that data.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-joins-and-validation?u=76281980&t=413)** If you're a data analyst you'll have plenty of opportunity to practice this in real life as a part of your everyday job.

### 4. Getting Started with a Data Project

#### Getting started with data projects
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here are a few best practices for any data analyst.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=4)** First of all, there is no guesswork in data.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=7)** If you do not know the answer, do not make one up.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=10)** I tell my new analysts that the day of guessing is officially over for them.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=15)** I encourage people to answer with things like, let me confirm, or I can get that information.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=21)** Don't send your manager off thinking one thing only to discover you were way off.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=27)** There is no dialing it back.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=29)** People will react to the information that you give them, and they believe you're providing them the answer, not a guess.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=37)** Don't publish a talking point with data until you have vetted it and verified it.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=43)** You can do a few of these verification steps yourself, and I'll share that a little bit later in this course, but it never hurts to have a peer review.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=51)** The more senior the people on your team, the more they'll appreciate the fact that you went that extra mile to ensure accuracy.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=58)** I'm sure all of us have been to meetings where people were not prepared for the meeting, and we need to use our time effectively because we don't have a lot of it.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=67)** It's so easy when everyone is together for all of that time to be gone.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=71)** Be sure you ask to be on the agenda.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=74)** Let people know you need just a few minutes to gain clarity or get feedback.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=78)** Take notes of everything in relationship to the objective.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=82)** Sometimes people who have the information are hard to reach, and when you have time with them, and they're giving you that information, be sure to make notes on it.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=92)** It can be frustrating when people come into meetings and they start presenting information and there's no setup and no context behind what they're presenting.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=101)** So I encourage people to provide some details about what you'll be presenting so that when they're getting the first view of it, they can understand it.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=109)** It's also helpful when you provide that detailed information, when they review it after the meeting, they can have that information as a primer before they dive in.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=118)** I like to use Word documents and simply list where the data came from, key definitions of things that they may see and other information that might be relevant for them so they can get the most out of the data.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=130)** I have been in so many meetings where data analysts just throw data up on the screen and dive in, and it will make the presentation way harder than it needs to be.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=140)** Do not eat up critical time that you need for followup by just diving in on screen.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=145)** When you have a lot of ground to cover, use a slide deck to help guide you through.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=150)** That deck will keep you on track.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=152)** It also ensures that you've covered all of your key points without getting sidelined by people looking at your data as you've just dove in on the screen.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=161)** I learned pretty early on in my career that people need time to process and digest the things that I'm showing them.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=168)** I have to reiterate the key points while they're looking at what's on my screen.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=172)** I remind presenters all the time, tell them what you're going to tell them, tell them and then tell them what you've just told them and give them time to understand and process so they can ask valid questions.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/getting-started-with-data-projects?u=76281980&t=184)** So my last best practice is keep in mind it's never a bad idea to ask other professionals their best practices.

#### Discovering common beginner mistakes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] People make mistakes.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=2)** I wanted to share with you where I recognize most mistakes being made by new data analysts.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=7)** When you're new to a role, you definitely want to impress, but this can be a dangerous misstep if you do not spend time learning about the data.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=15)** Anyone can make a Pivot Table show up or create calculations, but did you pay attention to how that data is structured and what it meant to the overall objective?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=26)** No matter how small the dataset or how large and complicated, please spend time looking over what the field headings are, what the data types may be and understanding of the values.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=38)** People not looking for duplicated data in a set is a major issue.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=43)** Again, if you spend a little bit of time up front, you'll be able to spot those.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=47)** However, the bigger the dataset, the harder it is to detect duplicates.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=52)** It's impossible to detect them if you're not looking for them.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=55)** There isn't a single dataset that I work with that I do not sum the numbers, average them and count them.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=61)** It gives me a lot of information up front that I need later to verify my records.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=67)** Record counts of each dataset that you work with are invaluable to you as you begin to work with the data.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=73)** If you're joining data with queries, you must know the record counts to validate your dataset.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=78)** If I have 50 product records and 1,000 sales records and I'm producing a sales order report, there's never going to be more than 1,000 sales.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=87)** And if all the sudden I see 50,000 sales records, I immediately know I have an issue.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=93)** Time is valuable and it's a non-renewable resource.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=97)** When you know that your time is limited with decision makers be sure you have your questions documented ahead of time.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=104)** That way you know all the questions you need to cover.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=107)** Also document the answers you've received, and follow up with confirmation that you have the correct understanding.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=114)** I think one of the scariest things for me as a data analyst is knowing that anyone can make numbers show up and that they might appear to be correct.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=123)** I would ensure before you go to the annual meeting and you've just told everyone that this is the best year of record, or the worst ever, that you've done some form of verification of your calculations.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=135)** You want to make sure that what you're showing is correct.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=138)** From the newest analyst to the most senior, you must verify your numbers and calculations.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=144)** Keep in mind that logical errors do not produce an error message.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=148)** They're just incorrect.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=149)** So take that extra time to verify your work.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=153)** It's hard though, because sometimes people have been encouraged not to ask questions.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=158)** I believe that this happens based on the types of questions people have asked over the years.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=162)** However, if you have critically considered your question and you know you need an answer from an authority, then you definitely need to ask.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=170)** Don't guess the answer.

> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=172)** People will often provide you what they think you need but not always everything you need.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=178)** Be sure that you've asked for documentation.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=180)** It could be that no one has ever asked for that documentation before.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=184)** If it exists, you'll work smarter and not harder.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=187)** And if it doesn't exist, then you've lost nothing.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=190)** And if you create it, then you're providing even more value than just the data.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=196)** I think sometimes people think that they just need to jump in, and they don't spend enough time analyzing existing reports.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=203)** There is literally nothing wrong with looking at system reports.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=206)** I did it for years, and in fact I encourage people to do it to save time.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=211)** The people who built the system are typically responsible for what we call canned reports.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=216)** There's a lot of value in understanding how the developers joined all the pieces together.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=222)** Also reviewing the reports of others, if they're willing to share.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=225)** Understand if they're not sharing, it's likely the fear of someone else peeking over their shoulder.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=230)** People are thrust into report writing regardless of skills.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=234)** So it may be they're hesitant to share because they're scared of what you'll think about their data.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=238)** Just carefully ask for information that might be useful to you as a new person to this data.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/discovering-common-beginner-mistakes?u=76281980&t=243)** If you can avoid mistakes by leaning on what others have already learned over time, you will definitely come in more confident about your work overall.

#### Maintaining original data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] In a dream world, we would run quality data sets from our systems, but that's not always the case.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=6)** We might only receive a spreadsheet or have limited access to export.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=11)** In some cases we'll receive a CSV file.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=15)** It is important to maintain the original data that you've exported or received.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=19)** It gives you two things, really the ability to restart without having to start all the way back over and also provide you an audit trail.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=28)** Let's talk about a real life example.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=30)** You don't have full access to run your own record set for monthly reports, you receive it from the DBAs and the IT department, they run it and send it to you.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=40)** You then do your cleanup processes, write your reports and then deliver them to your team month by month.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=47)** In a scenario like this, I keep the file that was delivered to me in a dedicated folder for these types of requests, I do not rely just on my email as my source.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/maintaining-original-data?u=76281980&t=57)** I typically start by creating a folder called Original Data and I'll do this now in my folder in chapter four in the 04_05.
>

#### Understanding truths
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=0)** - The moment I learned there are multiple truths around data in my career, I became a better analyst.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=6)** To keep it simple, there are three truths, the stats truth, the data truth and the business truth.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=13)** The stats truth at a minimum is the significance of your results.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=17)** Not all my projects have a full data science team, but there is some work that absolutely requires that layer.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=24)** Data is given to statisticians and researchers for that stats truth.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=29)** Their truth is to present the statistical outcomes and the significance of the report and frankly tell you if your data is actually just a coincidence.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=38)** To learn more about stats and their application to data, check out Statistics Foundations by Eddie Davila.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=44)** The data truth can lead us to other truths.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=48)** I once took a report to a manager of the entire organization.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=52)** I was so proud of it.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=54)** It was data from four different systems that had been consolidated together for the very first time and they had never seen their data like that.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=63)** In those days that report took hours to run and when I delivered it to my boss, he said, this was great, but it's not accurate.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=72)** Oh, I couldn't believe it.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=73)** Did I just give my boss an inaccurate report?
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=77)** Did I tie the data wrong?
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=79)** But I said, it's in your systems.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=82)** The data truth can be impacted by the business roles and in my story that's exactly what happened.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=87)** Not all the data had been collected yet.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=90)** And because of that, it was impacting the truth.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=93)** Just remember, in data, timing is everything.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=97)** Then there are moments where the data truth is skewed by the way the data was tied together.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=102)** That was the first thing I checked.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=104)** This is why joins are so important.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=106)** You can never spend enough time learning about joins and practicing them.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=111)** Then there are some basic truths.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=112)** For example, we have a list of unique products.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=115)** Let's say that there are 300 individual products and that's the truth, but that really just tells us the count not if they count because maybe not all of them have been ordered, which leads us to the business truth.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=129)** Likely the entire reason you're working with data is to get to the business truth.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=134)** The data truth and the stats truth inform the business truth.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=138)** Companies that produce, their business truth is a measure of their production.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=143)** The data truth can work with the business truth to inform and create better processes and give them the ability to see and create KPIs, which are key performance indicators.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=153)** So for example, if we spend hours on the development of a product and hold that inventory and it never sells, the data and the business truth tell us it's likely not worth it to make that product.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=166)** All of these truths together are meant to develop measures to improve outcomes.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-truths?u=76281980&t=171)** As an analyst, you need to be aware of all the truths and make sure you're speaking to the truth of not only the actual data, but the significance of the data and how it informs the truth of the business.

### 5. Data Importing, Exporting, and Connections

#### Learning about data governance
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] No two organizations are exactly the same, and certain organizations have to adhere to regulations and compliance.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=7)** For example, public companies in the United States must be compliant with Sarbanes-Oxley, or SOX.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=13)** In Canada, they have a version of this called C-SOX, And in Japan, it's called J-SOX.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=18)** No matter the country or the name of it, it's all around compliance, and data is certainly part of the compliance process.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=25)** I'm certainly not an expert on all things data governance, but I am a data analyst, and I'm impacted by the rules that are set by the governance plans.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=33)** Whether or not an organization is required by law to follow certain data governance, they are likely to have a living, breathing data plan that has controls in place.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=44)** I want to talk about these plans referred to as data governance and how it impacts you as an analyst.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=50)** Data governance is more than just the actual data.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=53)** It includes processes, technology, and people.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=57)** Key goals for governance are trustworthy datasets that are understandable, correct, secure.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=62)** And one of the key outcomes is high quality data.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=66)** When exploring data governance inside an organization, one of the first areas to explore is the ownership of the data.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=73)** Different areas of an organization collect different data.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=77)** The way they collect it may be different from the other areas of the organization, and the softwares they use may be different.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=84)** Here's an example.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=85)** Salespeople in the organization, they use a software called CRM, or customer relationship management.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=93)** Their data is used by other areas of the organization, but it's the sales group that have ownership of these data.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=100)** The group that owns the data is likely to be involved in your access to that data, even if IT is the gatekeeper that establishes your permission to that data.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=111)** The IT group or technology department will also have plans in place to secure the data, and they are required to maintain certain forms of compliance themselves.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=120)** It's important to understand that the way you gain access to the data and what permissions you will be allowed is usually controlled by the data governance plan.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=128)** In some organizations, only people in IT have access to the backend of all the data systems.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=135)** They may or may not provide you some form of data warehouse to access to pull reports.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=141)** Regardless of the systems in place or the people involved, if you have some form of data either directly or indirectly, you can build processes to work with that data.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=151)** Keep great notes of where the data is coming from and how you gained access to it.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=156)** You can always work within the constraints of the rules.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=158)** It's just not always elegant.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=160)** I support a company that, when we're working with their accounting systems, we discovered that because of the critical data in that system, the rules are you either have access to all of it, or you have access to none of it.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=173)** By leveraging what we know about data, we're able to automate datasets so that people who need access to the data but not access to the system still have protected data.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=183)** I found early in my career the more critical my reporting was to the decision-making, the more access I gained to data.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=191)** Keep in mind, when you try to pick up the latest, greatest tools to deal with data, you really need a broad range of skills and tools because you don't always know exactly how you'll be working with any given dataset.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=203)** It's also worth noting that organizations are sometimes early in their adoption of data governance.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=208)** New companies, of course, are going to be new to data governance, and older companies that haven't been under high regulation are also still developing their data governance plans.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=219)** To protect yourself and your organization, you do not need all permissions to all data.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=226)** When I work with a company I only ask for access to the data that I need, and I only asked for read access.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=233)** I don't need access to their entire data infrastructure.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=236)** "And certainly, don't make me a global admin," the joke typically comes out of my mouth.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=241)** Don't put me on the list of potential offenders, because if I don't need the access to the data because I'm not using it, then it's just a layer of protection for me and, well, for them.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=252)** There's certainly more to learn about data governance, but one of the key outcomes is the quality of the data.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=259)** We're all looking for high-quality data.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/learning-about-data-governance?u=76281980&t=261)** That way, when we build reports or visuals, we can feel more confident about what we're reporting.

#### Understanding source data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It is so important to understand source data, and it's also important to understand the source of the data you're working with.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=9)** Where did it come from and how did you receive it?
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=12)** Systems that people use at work are often powered by databases or capture data and place it into databases.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=20)** In a perfect world, we would always be working with the source of the data.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=25)** Sometimes working with the source is a challenge due to data governance, or even how it's structured in a system.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=33)** Source data is where data is first initiated, and the hope is that is the most accurate compared with maybe say second-hand data.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=43)** In today's environment, with needing data from multiple sources, you may find you're working with data that's stored in a data warehouse or some other data system that ensures you have the datasets you might need.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=57)** This may come from another person who has access to the original source data, or you may be able to export it out of a system.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=65)** But in the end, it's all part of the source data.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=69)** I think one of the top skills of a data analyst or data worker of almost any type is the ability to work with different sources of data and connect them for further analysis, reporting, or visualization.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=82)** It's important to always keep note of where your source data comes from, and if it's coming directly from the source of the original source, or if it's a pass-through to you, meaning someone sent it to you from the source.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=95)** It's also important to know that the same data can live in different sources.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=100)** Let me give you an example.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=102)** We have a company that we write reports for and they have a human resources system and a separate payroll system.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=110)** More specifically, when an employee hires onto the organization, the very first place you find them is captured in the HR system.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=120)** This is where the employee ID is created, and this employee ID is used in other systems like payroll and benefits and even technology systems.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=130)** The HR system keeps up with the necessary HR information, but it isn't specific enough for payroll.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=138)** It just supplies information to the payroll system.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=141)** This makes sure that employees get their paychecks.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=145)** Because the paycheck data is in a dedicated system, you will not find paychecks in HR.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=151)** You'll find employee information from HR in the payroll system, but the primary source for paycheck data is the payroll system.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=160)** Let me take it a step further.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=162)** In our example, human resources is one department, and they're responsible for all the data in HR, and providing necessary information to accounting, which is responsible in par for the payroll data.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=176)** If you work in neither of those departments and you've been tasked with reporting on hiring information and performance bonuses, then you need information from both source systems.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=189)** HR data and payroll data are highly protected data structures, and for what you need, they may not give you direct access to both systems.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=198)** They will likely provide you just the data you need in the form of CSV.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=204)** When you report on the data, your source is the CSV that is provided from both departments, and the sources of those CSV files are from the two source systems.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=216)** Knowing your sources will help you explain how you got your data and where it is originating from.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=223)** This is important for multiple reasons.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=225)** You may detect an error and you need to know who to tell.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=228)** Sometimes systems get upgraded and changed and impacts your data.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=233)** If something is off in your data, you need a path to follow to determine the issue.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-source-data?u=76281980&t=238)** It's always important to keep note of where your source data comes from, and if it comes directly from the source or if it's a pass-through to you.

#### Working with flat files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] People are not always able to directly connect to the backend of a database system and sometimes they're limited to exports of the data or they receive files that are in Excel format or CSV.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=12)** I would refer to these as flat files and they're disconnected from the data.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=16)** So what do I mean by flat or disconnected data?
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=20)** You're not directly connected to the system that houses that data which means when the data in the system is updated, your data in that flat file is not updated.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=29)** You would need another file to capture those data changes.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=33)** CSV common flat files.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=36)** CSV stands for comma separated values.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=39)** With a CSV it's a delimited file, and the comma serves as the delimiter.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=44)** What that delimiter does is separates each of the columns from each other.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/working-with-flat-files?u=76281980&t=48)** There's also other types like tab delimited or fixed width, which isn't a delimiter but an amount of space.
>

### 6. Getting Started with Data Cleaning and Modeling

#### Understanding ETL in data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I can remember the first time I heard about data warehousing.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=3)** I wanted to go learn about it.

>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=7)** They were talking about normalizing data and then denormalizing it and storing it somewhere else.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=13)** I found this interesting because I was already building little data warehouses, and now that process had a name.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=20)** My customers needed to run reports on a routine and they needed to have meaningful data without having me recreate it all the time.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=28)** I wasn't using super fancy tools.
>

>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=33)** I say that to say don't overthink all of this.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=37)** You're going to hear a million different terms and thoughts on data over your career, but at this point, your focus should be learning how to get the data you have to a point that's readable and meaningful.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=48)** The process of getting data from the source, making it meaningful, and placing it out for others to use or read is referred to is ETL.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=57)** ETL is also a critical process of data warehousing.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=61)** ETL stands for extract, transform, and load.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=65)** I've seen other variations of this acronym, like extraction, transformation, and loading, and what I found is, just like me, there are a lot of business users who are doing ETL.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=76)** They're doing ETL-like processes to achieve their reports, except maybe they're not flooding data warehouses.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=82)** They're just building weekly or monthly reports.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=85)** If you want to learn more about ETL, just simply search the library for the acronym ETL, and you'll learn a lot about it.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=94)** When I think about ETL as a more universal concept, it really is applicable to any data.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=100)** Now, the transformation, that's the T in ETL, that's where the cleaning, and standardization, and addressing the data we don't have occurs.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/understanding-etl-in-data?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's dive into the cleaning and modeling processes using various tools and we'll get into that T for transforming data.

#### Changing case and replace values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We change case and data for a million reasons.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=3)** For example, we might need to uppercase two letter abbreviations for state, or maybe names have been entered and sometimes they're uppercase and sometimes they're lower case and we want to convert them to proper case.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=15)** We're usually doing this for consistency across our data.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=19)** It's also important to note that Power Query is case sensitive.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=22)** So if we're going to use tools like replace values sometimes to make it simple it's easier to convert to a different case.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=31)** In our example here, we want to actually uppercase first name and last name.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/changing-case-and-replace-values?u=76281980&t=35)** So let's go take a look at our data.
>

>

> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/learning-data-analytics-1-foundations/creating-logical-functions?u=76281980&t=158)** If you've ever built an If function, you can see how this is a simple way to create conditional variables in your data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **CLI Commands:** make (4)
> **Code Keywords:** function (2), let (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)
